<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="analytical-balance">ANALYTICAL BALANCE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An analytical balance is a highly accurate laboratory balance designed to determine an object's mass precisely. The objects could be solid, liquid, granular substances, or powders. It is also known as laboratory balance which is class II balance.</p>
<p>The balance is extremely sensitive since it provides precise measurement having readability of up to 0.00001 grams (0.01 mg), and is often used in labs. It detects the weight of an object of 100g to within ±</p>
<p>0.01 mg.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="principle-of-analytical-balance">Principle of Analytical Balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principle-of-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Analytical balance calculates weights based on the force required to balance the mass of a sample rather than utilizing actual masses. They produce a force to balance the sample using an electromagnet, then output the result by measuring the force required. A transparent enclosure with doors surrounds the measurement pan of an analytical balance (0.1 mg or greater), blocking external influences. As a result, dust does not collect, and air currents in the room do not affect the weighing performance.</p>
<p>Analytical balance works on the principle of "magnetic force restoration." It is an electromagnetic balance that measures the mass of an object using an electromagnet. These balances do not directly measure the mass; rather, they measure the force that acts in the downwards direction on the balancing pan. An electromagnetic motor measures this force and ultimately determines the mass of the object. The motor requires an electric current for this purpose, and this current is directly proportional to the weight of the object and thus can be used to measure the object's mass. This mass is displayed on the balance screen. Many balances possess a "null detector" that uses a light source to indicate when the object's weight is equal to the electromagnetic forces</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="parts-of-analytical-balance">Parts of analytical balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#parts-of-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-b765b6b1.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg"></p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING | TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1
COMPILED BY A. O. ONYANGO
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
48</p>
<p>Balance plate (Pan)- A container to hold the sample material for mass measurement.</p>
<p>Weights- It enables the calibration of analytical scales.</p>
<p>Power button (on/off button)- It is used to activate or deactivate the balance.</p>
<p>'Re-zero' or 'Tare' button- It is used to rebalance the system and bring it back to neutral (zero)</p>
<p>'Mode' button- It is used to configure the measurement conversion system such that the conversion system can be changed as necessary.</p>
<p>Draft shields- These are incorporated into the design of analytical balances to offer protection from outside factors such as air flows and dust that could compromise precision.</p>
<p>Level adjustment feet- These enable the balance to be brought to the reference position. These are movable legs. The leveling bubble, spirit level, or plumb bob determines the reference position.</p>
<p>Level indicator- It checks the balance in level.</p>
<p>Display panel- It displays various information such as results, errors, information for function settings, and function in progress.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="weighing-procedure-of-an-analytical-balance">Weighing Procedure of an Analytical Balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#weighing-procedure-of-an-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-select-a-proper-location">1. Select a proper location<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-select-a-proper-location" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Several external environmental factors influence the measurement. Hence, it's necessary to carry out the weighing procedures in a suitable location.</p>
<p>Choose a stable and horizontal location free from external disturbances.</p>
<p>Avoid direct sunlight and make sure there are no extreme temperature changes.</p>
<p>Refrain from touching magnetic or magnetic field-generating objects or equipment.</p>
<p>The environment should be as dust-free as possible.</p>
<p>Steer clear of air currents produced by ventilators, air conditioners, open doors, and windows.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-leveling-the-analytical-balance">2. Leveling the analytical balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-leveling-the-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Repeatable measurements and precise findings require precise horizontal positioning.</p>
<p>The analytical balance must be leveled to account for any slight deviations or tilts at this site.</p>
<p>Until the air bubble in the indicator is in the center, the analytical balance's leveling feet should be adjusted.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="3-calibrating-the-analytical-balance">3. Calibrating the analytical balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-calibrating-the-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1
COMPILED BY A. O. ONYANGO
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
49</p>
<ol>
<li>The analytical balance must be calibrated for the sample to be precisely weighed. The following circumstances call for calibrating the operations:</li>
</ol>
<p>Modifications to the usage location (including moving within the same room).</p>
<p>Alteration in the environment.</p>
<p>Before each use.</p>
<p>In-built internal calibration or external calibration can be performed. Internal calibration requires manual input from the users to reset the analytical balance, and external calibration is performed using a calibration pendulum.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-weighing">4. Weighing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-weighing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is preferable to preheat it for an hour before using it.</p>
<p>Set the analytical balance to zero in the no-load condition by pressing the “tare” button.</p>
<p>Place the weigh boat, weigh paper, or other vessel or container in the center of the weighing pan and then shut the glass door of the weighing chamber.</p>
<p>Check the value that was displayed after it was stabilized. The appearance of the stability mark indicates a stable state.</p>
<p>To exclude container mass from the measurement, the ‘TARE’ button is pressed to reset the mass to zero.</p>
<p>Add the substance to be weighed after removing the container from the balance. Avoid putting things in the balance pan because doing so can contaminate the balance.</p>
<p>Reset the container’s balance, then wait 5-10 seconds (up to a minute) for the mass reading to stabilize. 5. Cleaning</p>
<p>The analytical balance’s measurement accuracy and lifespan are improved by routine cleaning.</p>
<p>Use just a piece of lint-free, soap-wet, mild detergent-coated cloth to clean the analytical balance.</p>
<p>Avoid using any abrasive or harsh cleaning chemicals as well as organic solvents.</p>
<p>Cut off the electricity and unplug the power cord while cleaning.</p>
<p>Ensure no liquid or dust gets inside the housing of the analytical balance.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-analytical-balance">Types of Analytical Balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-two-pan-or-equal-arm-analytical-balance">1. Two pan or equal arm analytical balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-two-pan-or-equal-arm-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>It works on the principle of a first-class lever. The fulcrum serves as a beam over a centered pivot joint to which two pans are attached. Two pans and the central beam are placed in three</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1
COMPILED BY A. O. ONYANGO
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
50</p>
<p>prism-formed knife edges. Two pans are balanced against each other in which the object to be weighed is placed on one pan while the known weight is placed on the other. The two sources of error in two pan balance are:</p>
<ol>
<li>The two arms must be equidistant from the center, which is tedious, time-consuming, and difficult in practice. When an arm is only <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>5</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{-5}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">−</span><span class="mord mtight">5</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> longer than the other arm, it results in an error of <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>5</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{-5}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">−</span><span class="mord mtight">5</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> of the weight.</li>
<li>As the weight increases, the beam may gently flex over the knife edges, causing minuscule measurement inaccuracies.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-single-pan-or-unequal-arm-mechanical-balance">2. Single pan or unequal arm mechanical balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-single-pan-or-unequal-arm-mechanical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This balance overcomes the limitations of two pan balances. Two knife edges with unequal balance arms are present. The shorter arm supports the balance pan and a full complement of weights, and the longer arm contains a constant counterweight built into the balance beam.</p>
<p>Here, the empty pan is loaded with the object, and weights are removed from the shorter arm to compensate for the weight of the object loaded. Such a type of weighing is known as weighing by substitution.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-electronic-single-pan-balance">3. Electronic single pan balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-electronic-single-pan-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is also known as the electromagnetic force balance and has widely replaced the mechanical or single pan balance. The passing current generates a magnetic force that balances the load placed on the balance pan. Thus, the current required is directly proportional to the object's mass on the pan.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-microbalance">4. Microbalance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-microbalance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It can measure samples to a resolution of at least 1 million parts per gram. To precisely quantify tiny amounts of a sample, microbalances are employed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-affecting-the-performance-of-an-analytical-balance">Factors affecting the performance of an analytical balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-affecting-the-performance-of-an-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-temperature">1. Temperature<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-temperature" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A slight change in temperature results in a noticeable fluctuation of measurement. An increase in room temperature causes the expansion of the sample or loss of water from the sample, i.e., evaporation. Conversely, lowering the room temperature causes the contraction of the sample or condensation of water vapor.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-vibrations">2. Vibrations;<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-vibrations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1

51</p>
<p>Disturbances from refrigerators, ventilation systems, or other equipment alter the precision of the balance as a result of rearrangement, dislodgement, or spilling of the sample, thereby influencing the amount of material available and its distribution.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-chemical-reaction">3. Chemical Reaction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-chemical-reaction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Highly reactive samples are highly sensitive to atmospheric alterations. Thus, the samples to be weighed should remain chemically inert.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-air-currents">4. Air currents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-air-currents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The sample should be weighed in a well-ventilated room to ensure precise measurement and safety of lab workers as violent air currents result in a change in air pressure and hence, a misalignment in the sensitive support. 5. Calibration</p>
<p>An accurate reading by an analytical balance is provided when the instrument is calibrated. Some of these instruments have in-built internal calibration features that conduct calibration tests themselves with minimal contribution from the user. However, some require certified calibration masses and external effort from the user to determine calibration settings based on the specific lab environment. 6. Magnet</p>
<p>Inaccurate reading during sample weighing could be attributed to the nature of the samples. If the sample is magnetic or is magnetized, it may attract other elements or create a magnetic field that influences the sensor coil of the balance.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-fingerprints">7. Fingerprints<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-fingerprints" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If the sample size is very small (approximately 0.1 mg), fingerprints may alter the results.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages-of-analytical-balance">Advantages of Analytical Balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages-of-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A very high degree of accuracy</li>
<li>It is simple, useful, quick, and efficient to determine an object’s mass.</li>
<li>The self-calibration system permits a weight adjustment mechanism and ensures that the scale is always correctly set and produces the most precise results.</li>
<li>These balances can detect the smallest weight variations to the nearest decimal point.</li>
<li>It assists laboratories in upholding and enhancing acceptable good laboratory practices (GLPs).</li>
<li>The measurement through analytical balance ensures uniformity, consistency, reliability, quality, reproducibility, and integrity.</li>
<li>Limitations of Analytical Balances</li>
</ul>

<li>The accuracy and precision of the balance rely on environmental factors where the weighing is performed. Thus, fluctuation in readings can be seen.</li>
<li>It's not intended for use in rigorous measurements.</li>

<li>LCDs can be easily damaged due to heavy loads.</li>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="uses-applications-of-analytical-balance">Uses/ Applications of Analytical Balance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uses-applications-of-analytical-balance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="maintenance-and-cleaning-of-analytical-balances">Maintenance And Cleaning of Analytical Balances<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#maintenance-and-cleaning-of-analytical-balances" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Analytical balances are delicate instruments designed to measure small quantities of substances with precision. They are commonly used in scientific research, pharmaceutical industries, quality control laboratories, and more. Proper maintenance and cleaning of these balances are necessary to maintain their accuracy and functionality over time.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="importance-of-maintenance-and-cleaning">Importance of Maintenance and Cleaning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#importance-of-maintenance-and-cleaning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Regular maintenance and cleaning of Analytical Balances offer several benefits. Firstly, it ensures the accuracy and reliability of measurements. Dust, debris, or contamination can affect</p>

<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
53</p>
<p>the balance's performance and lead to erroneous readings. Secondly, maintenance helps identify any mechanical or electrical issues early on, allowing for timely repairs and minimizing downtime. Lastly, proper care and cleaning extend the lifespan of the balance, maximizing return on investment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="precautions-before-cleaning">Precautions Before Cleaning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#precautions-before-cleaning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Before initiating the cleaning process, it is crucial to take certain precautions to prevent damage to the analytical balance:</p>
<p>Power Off: Always turn off the balance and disconnect it from the power source before cleaning.</p>
<p>Protective Gear: Wear appropriate personal protective equipment, such as gloves and goggles, to safeguard yourself from chemicals or cleaning agents.</p>
<p>Manufacturer's Guidelines: Refer to the manufacturer's guidelines and user manual for specific instructions on cleaning and maintenance procedures. Adhering to these guidelines ensures you don't void any warranties or cause damage.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-100644b9.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cleaning-the-external-parts">Cleaning the External Parts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cleaning-the-external-parts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The external parts including the weighing pan, display panel, and keypad, should be regularly cleaned to maintain hygiene and prevent cross-contamination. Here's a step-by-step guide:</p>
<p>Use a soft brush or compressed air to remove any loose dust or debris from the weighing pan and surrounding areas.</p>
<p>Prepare a mild cleaning solution using a non-abrasive, non-reactive detergent and distilled water.</p>
<p>Moisten a lint-free cloth with the cleaning solution and gently wipe the external parts, ensuring no liquid enters the balance housing or electronic components.</p>
<p>Dry the surfaces thoroughly with a clean, lint-free cloth or allow them to air dry completely before reconnecting the power.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="cleaning-the-weighing-chamber">Cleaning the Weighing Chamber<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cleaning-the-weighing-chamber" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The weighing chamber is the enclosed area where the sample and weights are placed for measurement. Cleaning the weighing chamber is vital to prevent contamination and maintain accuracy. Follow these steps for effective cleaning:</p>
<ul>
<li>Remove any residual materials from the weighing chamber using a soft brush or vacuum cleaner designed for sensitive equipment.</li>
<li>Prepare a cleaning solution by diluting a mild detergent recommended by the manufacturer in distilled water.</li>
<li>Dampen a lint-free cloth with the cleaning solution and wipe the inner surfaces of the weighing chamber, including the walls, windows, and draft shield.</li>
<li>Thoroughly rinse the cloth to remove any detergent residue and repeat the wiping process to ensure all contaminants are removed.</li>
<li>Allow the weighing chamber to dry completely before using the balance.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-564650e1.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="handling-and-storage">Handling and Storage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#handling-and-storage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Proper handling and storage of analytical balances contribute to their longevity and accuracy. Consider the following tips:</p>
<ul>
<li>Avoid Overloading: Never exceed the balance's maximum capacity to prevent damage to internal components.</li>
<li>Handle with Care: Always handle the balance gently to avoid shocks or impacts that could disrupt its calibration.</li>
<li>Safe Storage: Store the balance in a clean, dry area with controlled temperature and humidity levels. Protect it from excessive heat, direct sunlight, and chemicals that may cause corrosion.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="regular-calibration">Regular Calibration<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#regular-calibration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Regular calibration is crucial to maintain the accuracy of analytical balances. Calibration ensures that the laboratory balance provides precise measurements within a specific tolerance range. It involves comparing the balance's readings to reference weights. Consult the manufacturer's guidelines or hire a professional calibration service to calibrate the balance at regular intervals.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="microscope">MICROSCOPE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>A microscope is a magnifying instrument. It is used in laboratories for viewing micro-organism, which cannot be viewed with naked eyes.</li>
<li>Is an instrument or equipment used to view objects and areas of objects that cannot be seen with the naked eye.</li>
<li>Microscopes are devices that allow the observer to an exceedingly close view of minute particles.</li>
<li>A microscope is an optical instrument that uses visible light and single or multiple lenses to magnify small objects</li>
</ul>
<p>Microscopy is the technical field of using microscopes to view objects and areas of objects that cannot be seen with the naked eye (objects that are not within the resolution range of the normal eye). There are three well-known branches of microscopy:</p>
<ul>
<li>Optical/ Light microscopy.</li>
<li>Electron microscopy.</li>
<li>Scanning probe microscopy.</li>
</ul>
<p>Microscopy covers a wide range of different techniques - what they have in common is that they produce images of objects at greater than life size - i.e. magnification is involved.</p>
<p>Magnification, M, is defined as the ratio of the size of a feature in the image to the size of the corresponding feature in the object:</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi><mo>=</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm {M} = \mathrm {D} / \mathrm {d}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathrm">M</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord mathrm">D</span><span class="mord">/</span><span class="mord mathrm">d</span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p>(where D is the size of feature in the image and d the size of feature in object).</p>
<p>There are two basic configurations of the conventional optical microscope, the simple (one lens) and compound (many lenses). The vast majority of modern microscopes are compound microscopes while some cheaper commercial digital microscopes are simple single lens microscopes. A magnifying glass is, in essence, a basic single lens microscope. In general microscope optics are static; to focus at different focal depths the lens to sample distance is adjusted and to get a wider or narrower field of view a different magnification objective lens must be used. Most modern research microscopes also have a separate set of optics for illuminating the sample.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="types-of-microscopes">Types of Microscopes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-microscopes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Microbiologists use a variety of microscopes, each with specific advantages and limitations. Microscopes are of two categories.</p>
<p>a) Light Microscope: Magnification is obtained by a system of optical lenses using light waves. It includes (i) Bright field (ii) Dark field (iii) Fluorescence (iv) Phase contrast and (v) UV Microscope.</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING | TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1
COMPILED BY A. O. ONYANGO
This document is available on
5
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
57</p>
<p>b) Electron Microscope: A system of electromagnetic lenses and a short beam of electrons are used to obtain magnification. It is of two types: (I) Transmission electron microscope (TEM) (ii) Scanning electron microscope (SEM).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-light-microscopes">A) Light Microscopes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-light-microscopes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Any microscope that uses a source of light to create an image of the specimen and, essentially includes all compound and stereo microscopes. The most widely used microscope uses light to create images of light transmitting specimens which are projected onto either the eye or a camera. The key components are a light source, an optical system, a specimen holder and a recording medium.</p>
<p>Optical or light microscopy involves passing visible light transmitted through or reflected from the sample through a single lens or multiple lenses to allow a magnified view of the sample. The resulting image can be detected directly by the eye, imaged on a photographic plate, or captured digitally. The single lens with its attachments, or the system of lenses and imaging equipment, along with the appropriate lighting equipment, sample stage, and support, makes up the basic light microscope. The most recent development is the digital microscope, which uses a CCD camera to focus on the exhibit of interest. The image is shown on a computer screen, so eyepieces are unnecessary.</p>
<p>There are two basic types of optical microscopes: simple microscopes and compound microscopes. A simple microscope uses the optical power of single lens or group of lenses for magnification. A compound microscope uses a system of lenses (one set enlarging the image produced by another) to achieve much higher magnification of an object. The vast majority of modern research microscopes are compound microscopes while some cheaper commercial digital microscopes are simple single lens microscopes. Compound microscopes can be further divided into a variety of other types of microscopes which differ in their optical configurations, cost, and intended purposes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="simple-microscope">Simple microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#simple-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A simple microscope is a microscope that uses only one lens for magnification, and is the original design of light microscope. It consists of a small, single converging lens mounted on a brass plate, with a screw mechanism to hold the sample or specimen to be examined. The use of a single convex lens or groups of lenses are found in simple magnification devices such as the magnifying glass, loupes, and eyepieces for telescopes and microscopes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="simple-microscope-definition">Simple Microscope Definition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#simple-microscope-definition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1

<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
58</p>
<ul>
<li>A simple microscope is one that uses a single lens for magnification, such as a magnifying glass while a compound microscope uses several lenses to enhance the magnification of an object.</li>
<li>It uses a lens to enlarge an object through angular magnification alone, giving the viewer an erect enlarged virtual image.</li>
<li>The use of a single convex lens or groups of lenses is found in simple magnification devices such as the magnifying glass, loupes, and eyepieces for telescopes and microscopes.</li>
<li>It is actually a convex lens of small focal length, which is used for seeing the magnified images of small objects.</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="principle-of-simple-microscope">Principle of Simple Microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principle-of-simple-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
59</p>
<p>A simple microscope works on the principle that when a tiny object is placed within its focus, a virtual, erect and magnified image of the object is formed at the least distance of distinct vision from the eye held close to the lens.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="magnification-of-simple-microscope">Magnification of Simple Microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#magnification-of-simple-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The magnifying power of a simple microscope is given by:</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="bold">M</mi><mo>=</mo><mn mathvariant="bold">1</mn><mo>+</mo><mi mathvariant="bold">D</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathbf {M} = \mathbf {1} + \mathbf {D} / \mathbf {F}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6861em;"></span><span class="mord mathbf">M</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord mathbf">1</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">+</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord mathbf">D</span><span class="mord">/</span><span class="mord mathbf">F</span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p>Where D = the least distance of distinct vision</p>
<p>F = focal length of the convex lens</p>
<ul>
<li>The focal length of the convex lens should be small because smaller the focal length of the lens, greater will be its magnifying power.</li>
<li>The maximum magnification of a simple microscope is about 10, which means that the object will appear 10 times larger by using the simple microscope of maximum magnification.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="optical-parts">Optical parts:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#optical-parts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The optical parts of the microscope are used to view, magnify, and produce an image from a specimen placed on a slide. These parts are involved in passing the light through the object (specimen) and magnifying its size.</p>
<p>The components of the optical parts are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Mirror:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>A plano-convex mirror is fitted below the stage to the vertical rod by means of a frame.</li>
<li>It focuses the surrounding light on the object to be observed.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Lens:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>A biconvex lens is fitted above the stage, to the vertical rod, by means of a frame.</li>
<li>It magnifies the size of the object and the enlarged virtual image formed is observed by keeping the eye above it.</li>
<li>For proper focusing, the lens can be moved up and down by the frame.</li>
</ul>
<p>In case of microscopes with oil immersion, when light passes from a material of one refractive index to material of another, as from glass to air or from air to glass, it bends. The refractive</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1
COMPILED BY A. O. ONYANGO
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
60</p>
<p>index of air is 1.0, which is less than that glass slide (1.56). So, when light passes from glass (dense medium) to air (lighter medium), the rays get refracted, which led to loss of resolution of image. Light of different wavelengths bends at different angles, so that as objects are magnified the images become less and less distinct. This loss of resolution becomes very apparent at magnifications of above 400x or so. Even at 400x the images of very small objects are badly distorted. Placing a drop of oil (Cedar wood oil) with the same refractive index (1.51) as glass between the cover slip and objective lens eliminates two refractive surfaces and considerably enhances resolution, so that magnifications of 1000x or greater can be achieved. Oil immersion is essential for viewing individual bacterial cell. A disadvantage of oil immersion viewing is that the oil must stay in contact, and oil should be viscous.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="applications">Applications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#applications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It is usually used for the study of microscopic algae, fungi, and biological specimens.</li>
<li>It is commonly used by watchmakers to see the magnified view of small parts of a watch.</li>
<li>It is also used by the jewelers to see the magnified view of the fine parts of jewelry.</li>
<li>It is used to see the enlarged image of letters of a book, textures of fibers or threads of a cloth.</li>
<li>It is used to see the magnified view of different particles of different types of soils.</li>
<li>It is used by palmists to see an enlarged view of the lines of our hand.</li>
<li>It is used by skin specialists to find out various diseases of the skin.</li>
<li>It is also used to see the details of stamp and engravings.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="compound-microscope">Compound microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#compound-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The common light microscope used in the laboratory is called a compound microscope because it contains two types of lenses that function to magnify an object. There are two types of compound microscopes depending on the number of ocular lenses: Binocular and Monocular. The lens closest to the eye is called the ocular, while the lens closest to the object is called the objective. Most microscopes have on their base an apparatus called a condenser, which condenses light rays to a strong beam. A diaphragm located on the condenser controls the amount of light coming through it. Both coarse and fine adjustments are found on the light microscope.</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING | TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagram-of-a-compound-microscope">Diagram of a compound microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagram-of-a-compound-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A compound microscope uses a lens close to the object being viewed to collect light (called the objective lens) which focuses a real image of the object inside the microscope. That image is then magnified by a second lens or group of lenses (called the eyepiece) that gives the viewer an enlarged inverted virtual image of the object. The use of a compound objective/eyepiece combination allows for much higher magnification. Common compound microscopes often feature exchangeable objective lenses, allowing the user to quickly adjust the magnification. A compound microscope also enables more advanced illumination setups, such as phase contrast.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="parts-of-a-compound-microscope">Parts of a Compound Microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#parts-of-a-compound-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="eyepiece-and-body-tube">Eyepiece and Body Tube.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#eyepiece-and-body-tube" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The eyepiece is the lens through which the viewer looks to see the specimen.</li>
<li>It usually contains a 10X or 15X power lens.</li>
<li>The body tube connects the eyepiece to the objective lenses.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives-and-stage-clips">Objectives and Stage Clips<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives-and-stage-clips" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Objective Lenses are one of the most important parts of a Compound Microscope.</li>
<li>They are the closest to the specimen.</li>
<li>A standard Microscope has three to four Objective Lenses which range from 4X to 100X.</li>
<li>Stage Clips are metal clips that held the slide in a place.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="arm-and-base">Arm and Base<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#arm-and-base" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The Arm connects the Body Tube to the base of the Microscope.</li>
<li>The Base supports the Microscope and its where Illuminator.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="illuminator-and-stage">Illuminator and Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#illuminator-and-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The illuminator is the light source for a microscope.</li>
<li>A compound light microscope mostly uses a low voltage bulb as an illuminator.</li>
<li>The stage is the flat platform where the slide is placed.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nosepiece-and-aperture">Nosepiece and Aperture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nosepiece-and-aperture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Nosepiece is a rotating turret that holds the objective lenses.</li>
<li>The viewer spins the nosepiece to select different objective lenses.</li>
<li>The aperture is the middle of the stage that allows light from the illuminator to reach the specimen.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="condenser-iris-diaphragm-and-diaphragm">Condenser, Iris diaphragm, and Diaphragm<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#condenser-iris-diaphragm-and-diaphragm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A condenser gathers and focuses light from the illuminator onto the specimen being viewed.</li>
<li>Iris diaphragm adjusts the amount of light that reaches the specimen.</li>
<li>The diaphragm is a five holed disk placed under the stage.</li>
</ul>

<ul>
<li>Each hole is of a different diameter. By turning it, you can vary the amount of light passing through the stage opening.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="applications">Applications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#applications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A compound microscope is of great use in pathology labs so as to identify diseases.</li>
<li>Various crime cases are detected and solved by drawing out human cells and examining them under the microscope in forensic laboratories.</li>
<li>The presence or absence of minerals and the presence of metals can be identified using compound microscopes.</li>
<li>Students in schools and colleges are benefited by the use of a microscope for conducting their academic experiments.</li>
<li>It helps to see and understand the microbial world of bacteria and viruses, which is otherwise invisible to the naked eye.</li>
<li>Plant cells are examined and the microorganisms thriving on it can be ascertained with the help of a compound microscope. Thereby, a compound microscope has proved to be crucial to biologists.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Simplicity and its convenience.</li>
<li>A compound light microscope is relatively small, therefore it's easy to use and simple to store, and it comes with its own light source.</li>
<li>Because of their multiple lenses, compound light microscopes are able to reveal a great amount of detail in samples.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="working-principle-of-the-compound-microscope">Working Principle of the Compound Microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#working-principle-of-the-compound-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Compound microscopes have a combination of lenses that enhances both magnifying powers as well as the resolving power.</p>
<ul>
<li>The specimen or object, to be examined is usually mounted on a transparent glass slide and positioned on the specimen stage between the condenser lens and objective lens.</li>
<li>A beam of visible light from the base is focused by a condenser lens onto the specimen.</li>
</ul>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING | TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1
COMPILED BY A. O. ONYANGO
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
64</p>
<ul>
<li>The objective lens picks up the light transmitted by the specimen and creates a magnified image of the specimen called the primary image inside the body tube. This image is again magnified by the ocular lens or eyepiece.</li>
<li>When higher magnification is required, the nose piece is rotated after low power focusing to bring the objective of a higher power (generally 45X) in line with the illuminated part of the slide.</li>
<li>Occasionally very high magnification it required (e.g. for observing bacterial cell). In that case, an oil immersion objective lens (usually 100X) is employed.</li>
<li>The common light microscope is also called a bright-field microscope because the image is produced amidst a brightly illuminated field. The image appears darker because the specimen or object is denser and somewhat opaque than the surroundings. Part of the light passing through or object is absorbed.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="magnification-of-compound-microscope">Magnification of compound microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#magnification-of-compound-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In order to ascertain the total magnification when viewing an image with a compound light microscope, take the power of the objective lens which is at 4x, 10x or 40x and multiply it by the power of the eyepiece which is typically 10x.</p>
<p>Therefore, a 10x eyepiece used with a 40X objective lens will produce a magnification of 400X. The naked eye can now view the specimen at magnification 400 times greater and so microscopic details are revealed.</p>
<p>Alternatively, the magnification of the compound microscope is given by:</p>

<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="other-light-microscope-variants">Other light microscope variants<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#other-light-microscope-variants" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are many variants of the compound optical microscope design for specialized purposes. Some of these are physical design differences allowing specialization for certain purposes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Stereo microscope, a low-powered microscope which provides a stereoscopic view of the sample, commonly used for dissection.</li>
<li>Comparison microscope, which has two separate light paths allowing direct comparison of two samples via one image in each eye.</li>
<li>Inverted microscope, for studying samples from below; useful for cell cultures in liquid, or for metallography.</li>
<li>Fiber optic connector inspection microscope, designed for connector end-face inspection</li>
<li>Traveling microscope, for studying samples of high optical resolution</li>
</ul>
<p>Other light microscopes. In addition to the familiar compound microscope, microbiologists use other types of microscopes for specific purposes. These microscopes permit viewing of objects not otherwise seen with the light microscope.</p>
<p>An alternative microscope is the dark-field microscope, which is used to observe live spirochetes, such as those that cause syphilis. This microscope contains a special condenser that scatters light and causes it to reflect off the specimen at an angle. A light object is seen on a dark background.</p>
<p>A second alternative microscope is the phase-contrast microscope. This microscope also contains special condensers that throw light "out of phase" and cause it to pass through the object at different speeds. Live, unstained organisms are seen clearly with this microscope, and internal cell parts such as mitochondria, lysosomes, and the Golgi body can be seen with this instrument.</p>
<p>The fluorescent microscope uses ultraviolet light as its light source. When ultraviolet light hits an object, it excites the electrons of the object, and they give off light in various shades of color. Since ultraviolet light is used, the resolution of the object increases. A laboratory technique called the fluorescent-antibody technique employs fluorescent dyes and antibodies to help identify unknown bacteria.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="i-dark-field-microscope">I. DARK-FIELD MICROSCOPE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-dark-field-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In dark-field microscopy, specimen is brightly illuminated against a dark background. This type of microscope possesses a special type of condenser, which prevents the parallel and the oblique rays entering in to the objective and thus making the microscopic field dark. In the absence of specimen the entire field will appear as dark. In the presence of specimen, which differs in refractive index, the oblique rays are scattered by reflection and refraction and the scattered rays enter the objective making the specimen brightly illuminated.</p>
<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I TEACHING NOTES – VOLUME 1

<p>CLINICAL ENGINEERING I
66</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-f2790f44.jpeg" alt="img-32.jpeg">
Figure 14: Dark-Field microscope</p>
<p>Maximum magnification of 1500x and resolution of 0.1 – 0.2 µm can be obtained. It is useful in studying the morphology and motility of microorganisms. Dark field is especially useful for finding cells in suspension. Dark field makes it easy to obtain the correct focal plane at low magnification for small, low contrast specimens. The following are its uses:</p>
<p>a. Initial examination of suspensions of cells such as yeast, bacteria, small protists, or cell and tissue fractions including cheek epithelial cells, chloroplasts, mitochondria, even blood cells (small diameter of pigmented cells makes it tricky to find them sometimes despite the color).
b. Initial survey and observation at low powers of pond water samples, hay or soil infusions, purchased protist or metazoan cultures.
c. Examination of lightly stained prepared slides. Initial location of any specimen of very small size for later viewing at higher power.
d. Determination of motility in cultures</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ii-phase-contrast-microscope">II. PHASE CONTRAST MICROSCOPE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-phase-contrast-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In the 1930's Frederick Zernike devised a method of converting phase changes into differences in light intensity. This invention leads to the development of phase contrast microscope.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-cd861462.jpeg" alt="img-33.jpeg">
Figure 15: Phase contrast microscope</p>
<p>It has a special type of condenser, objective and a special magnifier. Light passing from one material into another of slightly different refractive index will undergo a change in the phase. This change in the phase of the light will in-turn increase the contrast. A system of rings in the condenser and objective separate the diffracted rays from the specimen. These diffracted rays from the specimen and the undiffracted rays combine and the phase difference is converted into difference in light intensity. In phase-contrast microscopy, unstained/living organisms can be examined. As a result, the internal contrast of various parts of a specimen against its surroundings is increased. It has a resolving power of <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>0.1</mn><mo>−</mo><mn>0.2</mn><mtext> </mtext><mi>μ</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">0.1 - 0.2\,\mu\mathrm{m}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">0.1</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">0.2</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span><span class="mord mathrm">m</span></span></span></span></span>.</p>
<p>To the left: Arrangement of the ring-shaped mask below the objective and of the phase-ring within the objective</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iii-uv-microscope">III. UV MICROSCOPE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-uv-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Resolution of a microscope depends upon the wavelength of light used. If, longer the wavelength of light used, lower will be the resolving power while shorter the wavelength, more will be the resolution. With this principle, UV rays of shorter wavelength are used as light source. Since UV rays can't penetrate the glass, quartz lenses are used. Since the UV rays are invisible, photographic plates should be used to record the image or special type of filters should be used to eliminate the UV rays from reaching the eyepiece. This is used in conjunction with fluorescent microscopy. Upon illumination with UV light certain fluorescent dyes emit light in visible range, which can be directly viewed.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-3f82b116.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg">
Figure 16: Working principles of UV microscope</p>
